a7:["$","hr",null,{"className":"my-10 border-[var(--border)]"}]
a8:["$","h2",null,{"children":"Transferable Patterns: Building Layered Permission Systems for AI Applications"}]
a9:["$","p",null,{"children":"The permission system design in Claude Code can be distilled into a set of general-purpose architectural patterns for AI application permissions. Here are the key design principles and their corresponding implementation strategies."}]
aa:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 1: Layered Evaluation, Deny First"}]
ac:["$","p",null,{"children":"Each layer does one thing, and the evaluation order is fixed. Deny rules come first to ensure the security baseline cannot be bypassed. This pattern can be directly applied to any AI application requiring permission control."}]
ad:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 2: Immutable State + Atomic Decisions"}]
ae:["$","p",null,{"children":["Permission state is protected with ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"DeepImmutable"}],", and the decision process is guaranteed atomic with ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"ResolveOnce"}],". When your system has multiple asynchronous sources that might simultaneously make decisions (users, automated systems, remote approvers), the ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"claim()"}]," pattern is a lightweight yet reliable solution."]}]
af:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 3: Secure Defaults + Explicit Relaxation"}]
b1:["$","p",null,{"children":"The system starts in its \"safest\" state. Every relaxation requires an explicit action — a user clicking \"Always allow,\" an admin configuring a policy, or a command-line argument being passed. This ensures security is never accidentally weakened due to missing configuration."}]
b2:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 4: Rule Source Tracking"}]
b3:["$","p",null,{"children":"Every rule carries source information (which configuration file, which layer). This is used not only for priority ordering but also for auditing — when a permission issue arises, you can pinpoint exactly \"where this rule came from.\""}]
b5:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 5: Handler Separation"}]
b6:["$","p",null,{"children":"Different runtime environments have different permission requirements. Claude Code's three handler patterns — interactive (competitive), coordinator (serial pre-screening), and swarm (mailbox forwarding) — demonstrate how to adapt a single rule system to different execution environments."}]
b7:["$","p",null,{"children":["The core abstraction is ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"PermissionContext"}],": it encapsulates all permission operations (logging, persistence, queue management), letting handlers focus solely on flow control. When adding a new runtime environment, you only need to implement a new handler function — no modifications to the rule evaluation logic are required."]}]
b8:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Principle 6: Progressive Trust"}]
b9:["$","p",null,{"children":["The ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"auto"}]," mode classifier doesn't call AI evaluation on every operation — it first uses fast paths to filter out clearly safe and clearly dangerous operations:"]}]
bb:["$","p",null,{"children":"Each additional fast-path layer eliminates a batch of unnecessary API calls. This pattern applies to any system that uses AI for runtime decision-making."}]
bc:["$","h3",null,{"children":"Practical Architecture Recommendations"}]
bd:["$","p",null,{"children":"If you're building a permission system for an AI application, you can start with this minimal architecture:"}]
bf:["$","p",null,{"children":"Then incrementally add features as needed:"}]
c0:["$","ul",null,{"children":["\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Immutable state protection"}],": Prevent runtime modifications"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Atomic racing"}],": When there are multiple asynchronous decision sources"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Source tracking"}],": When auditing or debugging is needed"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Classifier integration"}],": When the action space is too large for rules to cover"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Handler separation"}],": When there are multiple runtime environments"]}],"\n"]}]
c1:["$","hr",null,{"className":"my-10 border-[var(--border)]"}]
c2:["$","h2",null,{"children":"Conclusion"}]
c3:["$","p",null,{"children":["Claude Code's permission system is a multi-layered defense system. Its core insight is that ",["$","strong",null,{"children":"permissions are not a binary choice (allow/deny), but a continuous spectrum across multiple dimensions"}],"."]}]
c4:["$","ul",null,{"children":["\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Mode dimension"}],": From ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"bypassPermissions"}]," to ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"dontAsk"}],", users choose their own risk tolerance"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Rule dimension"}],": From blanket tool-level to content-level, supporting fine-grained permission control"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Source dimension"}],": From policies to sessions, multiple configuration layers stack on top of each other"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Role dimension"}],": The main agent, coordinator, and swarm workers each have distinct handling flows"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"children":[["$","strong",null,{"children":"Temporal dimension"}],": The classifier, Hooks, and user interaction race along the timeline, with the first to decide winning"]}],"\n"]}]
c5:["$","p",null,{"children":["The engineering implementation of this system contains many patterns worth learning: ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"DeepImmutable"}]," protects state safety, ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"ResolveOnce"}]," guarantees atomicity, handler separation ensures extensibility, and progressive fast paths reduce unnecessary computation. These patterns are not limited to permission systems — any system involving multi-source asynchronous decision-making can draw from them."]}]
c6:["$","p",null,{"children":["Finally, returning to the opening question: where should the permission boundary be drawn? Claude Code's answer is — ",["$","strong",null,{"children":"don't draw a fixed line; instead, provide a toolkit that lets each user draw their own"}],".
